<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Aggression</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Aggression_(disambiguation)" title="Aggression (disambiguation)">Aggression (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Aggressive" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Aggressive_(disambiguation)" title="Aggressive (disambiguation)">Aggressive (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Aggression</b> is overt, often harmful, social interaction with the intention of inflicting damage or other unpleasantness upon another individual. It may occur either in retaliation or without provocation. In humans, frustration due to blocked goals can cause aggression. Human aggression can be classified into direct and indirect aggression, whilst the first is characterized by physical or verbal behavior intended to cause harm to someone, the second one is characterized by a behavior intended to harm social relations of an individual or a group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In definitions commonly used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioral_science" title="Behavioral science">behavioral sciences</a>, aggression is a response by an individual that delivers something unpleasant to another person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Some definitions include that the individual must intend to harm another person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predatory" title="Predatory">Predatory</a> or defensive behavior between members of different species may not be considered aggression in the same sense.</p>
<p>Aggression can take a variety of forms, which may be expressed physically, or communicated <a href="/wiki/Verbal_aggressiveness" title="Verbal aggressiveness">verbally</a> or non-verbally: including anti-predator aggression, defensive aggression (fear-induced), predatory aggression, dominance aggression, inter-male aggression, resident-intruder aggression, maternal aggression, species-specific aggression, sex-related aggression, territorial aggression, isolation-induced aggression, irritable aggression, and brain-stimulation-induced aggression (hypothalamus). There are two subtypes of human aggression: (1) controlled-instrumental subtype (purposeful or goal-oriented); and (2) reactive-impulsive subtype (often elicits uncontrollable actions that are inappropriate or undesirable). Aggression differs from what is commonly called <a href="/wiki/Assertiveness" title="Assertiveness">assertiveness</a>, although the terms are often used interchangeably among laypeople (as in phrases such as "an aggressive salesperson").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aggression can have adaptive benefits or negative effects. Aggressive behavior is an individual or collective social interaction that may be defined in general terms as a hostile behavior with the intention of inflicting damage or harm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup> Two broad categories of aggression are commonly distinguished. One includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affective" title="Affective">affective</a> (emotional) and hostile, reactive, or <a href="/wiki/Revenge" title="Revenge">retaliatory</a> aggression that is a response to provocation, and the other includes instrumental, goal-oriented or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predatory" title="Predatory">predatory</a>, in which aggression is used as a mean to achieve a goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> An example of hostile aggression would be a person who punches someone who insulted him or her. An instrumental form of aggression would be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_robbery" title="Armed robbery">armed robbery</a>. Research on <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a> from a range of disciplines lend some support to a distinction between affective and predatory aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> However, some researchers question the usefulness of a hostile vs instrumental distinction in humans, despite its ubiquity in research, because most real-life cases involve mixed motives and interacting causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of classifications and dimensions of aggression have been suggested. These depend on such things as whether the aggression is verbal or physical; whether or not it involves <a href="/wiki/Relational_aggression" title="Relational aggression">relational aggression</a> such as covert bullying and social manipulation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> whether harm to others is intended or not; whether it is carried out actively or expressed passively; and whether the aggression is aimed directly or indirectly. Classification may also encompass aggression-related emotions (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a>) and mental states (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Impulsivity" title="Impulsivity">impulsivity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hostility" title="Hostility">hostility</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Aggression may occur in response to non-social as well as social factors, and can have a close relationship with stress coping style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Aggression may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Threat_display" title="Threat display">displayed</a> in order to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intimidate" title="Intimidate">intimidate</a>.</p>
<p>The operative definition of aggression may be affected by <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral</a> or <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> views. Examples are the axiomatic moral view called the <a href="/wiki/Non-aggression_principle" title="Non-aggression principle">non-aggression principle</a> and the political rules governing the behavior of one country toward another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Likewise in competitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sports" title="Sports">sports</a>, or in the <a href="/wiki/Workplace" title="Workplace">workplace</a>, some forms of aggression may be sanctioned and others not (see <a href="/wiki/Workplace_aggression" title="Workplace aggression">Workplace aggression</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Aggressive behaviors are associated with adjustment problems and several psychopathological symptoms such as Antisocial Personality Disorder, Borderline Personality Disorder, and Intermittent Explosive Disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biological approaches conceptualize aggression as an internal energy released by external stimuli, a product of evolution through natural selection, part of genetics, a product of hormonal fluctuations. Psychological approaches conceptualize aggression as a destructive instinct, a response to frustration, an affect excited by a negative stimuli, a result of observed learning of society and diversified reinforcement, a resultant of variables that affect personal and situational environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krah.C3.A92013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krah.C3.A92013-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Englander2003_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Englander2003-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term aggression comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>aggressio</i>, meaning attack. The Latin was itself a joining of <i>ad</i>- and <i>gradi</i>-, which meant step at. The first known use dates back to 1611, in the sense of an unprovoked attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> A psychological sense of "hostile or destructive behavior" dates back to a 1912 English translation of <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>'s writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Adler" title="Alfred Adler">Alfred Adler</a> theorized about an "aggressive drive" in 1908. <a href="/wiki/Parenting" title="Parenting">Child raising</a> experts began to refer to aggression, rather than anger, from the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethology">Ethology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ethology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg/250px-Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg/375px-Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg/500px-Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elephant_seal_fight_Part-1.jpg" title="Ampliar"></a></div>
Male <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">elephant seals</a> fighting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">Ethologists</a> study aggression as it relates to the interaction and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of animals in natural settings. In such settings aggression can involve bodily contact such as biting, hitting or pushing, but most conflicts are settled by threat displays and intimidating thrusts that cause no physical harm. This form of aggression may include the display of body size, antlers, claws or teeth; stereotyped signals including facial expressions; vocalizations such as bird song; the release of chemicals; and changes in coloration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The term <a href="/wiki/Agonistic_behaviour" title="Agonistic behaviour">agonistic behaviour</a> is sometimes used to refer to these forms of behavior.</p>
<p>Most ethologists believe that aggression confers biological advantages. Aggression may help an animal secure <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territory</a>, including resources such as food and water. Aggression between males often occurs to secure mating opportunities, and results in selection of the healthier/more vigorous animal. Aggression may also occur for self-protection or to protect offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Aggression between groups of animals may also confer advantage; for example, hostile behavior may force a population of animals into a new territory, where the need to adapt to a new environment may lead to an increase in genetic flexibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Between_species_and_groups">Between species and groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Between species and groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most apparent type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interspecific" title="Interspecific">interspecific</a> aggression is that observed in the interaction between a predator and its prey. However, according to many researchers, predation is not aggression. A cat does not hiss or arch its back when pursuing a rat, and the active areas in its <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> resemble those that reflect hunger rather than those that reflect aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> However, others refer to this behavior as predatory aggression, and point out cases that resemble hostile behavior, such as mouse-killing by rats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Aggressive_mimicry" title="Aggressive mimicry">aggressive mimicry</a> a predator has the appearance of a harmless organism or object attractive to the prey; when the prey approaches, the predator attacks.</p>
<p>An animal defending against a predator may engage in either "<a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight or flight</a>" in response to predator attack or threat of attack, depending on its estimate of the predator's strength relative to its own. Alternative defenses include a range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antipredator_adaptation" title="Antipredator adaptation">antipredator adaptations</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Alarm_signal" title="Alarm signal">alarm signals</a>. An example of an alarm signal is nerol, a chemical which is found in the mandibular glands of <i><a href="/wiki/Trigona_fulviventris" title="Trigona fulviventris">Trigona fulviventris</a></i> individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> Release of nerol by T. fulviventris individuals in the nest has been shown to decrease the number of individuals leaving the nest by fifty percent, as well as increasing aggressive behaviors like biting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> Alarm signals like nerol can also act as attraction signals; in T. fulviventris, individuals that have been captured by a predator may release nerol to attract nestmates, who will proceed to attack or bite the predator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aggression between groups is determined partly by willingness to fight, which depends on a number of factors including numerical advantage, distance from home territories, how often the groups encounter each other, competitive abilities, differences in body size, and whose territory is being invaded. Also, an individual is more likely to become aggressive if other aggressive group members are nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> One particular phenomenon – the formation of coordinated coalitions that raid neighbouring territories to kill <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecific" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a> – has only been documented in two species in the animal kingdom: <a href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">'common' chimpanzees</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Within_a_group">Within a group</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Within a group">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aggression between conspecifics in a group typically involves access to resources and breeding opportunities. One of its most common functions is to establish a <a href="/wiki/Dominance_hierarchy" title="Dominance hierarchy">dominance hierarchy</a>. This occurs in many species by aggressive encounters between contending males when they are first together in a common environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adamsonetal2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-adamsonetal2006-26">[26]</a></sup> Usually the more aggressive animals become the more dominant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In test situations, most of the conspecific aggression ceases about 24 hours after the group of animals is brought together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adamsonetal2006_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-adamsonetal2006-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Aggression has been defined from this viewpoint as "behavior which is intended to increase the social dominance of the organism relative to the dominance position of other organisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009-30">[30]</a></sup> Losing confrontations may be called <a href="/wiki/Social_defeat" title="Social defeat">social defeat</a>, and winning or losing is associated with a range of practical and psychological consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conflicts between animals occur in many contexts, such as between potential mating partners, between parents and offspring, between siblings and between competitors for resources. Group-living animals may dispute over the direction of travel or the allocation of time to joint activities. Various factors limit the escalation of aggression, including communicative displays, conventions and routines. In addition, following aggressive incidents, various forms of <a href="/wiki/Conflict_resolution" title="Conflict resolution">conflict resolution</a> have been observed in mammalian species, particularly in gregarious primates. These can mitigate or repair possible adverse consequences, especially for the recipient of aggression who may become vulnerable to attacks by other members of a group. Conciliatory acts vary by species and may involve specific gestures or simply more proximity and interaction between the individuals involved. However, conflicts over food are rarely followed by post conflict reunions, even though they are the most frequent type in foraging primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other questions that have been considered in the study of primate aggression, including in humans, is how aggression affects the organization of a group, what costs are incurred by aggression, and why some primates avoid aggressive behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Bonobo" title="Bonobo">bonobo chimpanzee</a> groups are known for low levels of aggression within a partially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matriarchal" title="Matriarchal">matriarchal</a> society. <a href="/wiki/Captivity_(animal)" title="Captivity (animal)">Captive</a> animals including primates may show abnormal levels of social aggression and self-harm that are related to aspects of the physical or social environment; this depends on the species and individual factors such as gender, age and background (e.g. raised wild or captive).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_explanations">Evolutionary explanations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolutionary explanations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Like many behaviors, aggression can be examined in terms of its ability to help an animal survive and reproduce, or alternatively to risk survival and reproduction. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cost-benefit_analysis" title="Cost-benefit analysis">cost-benefit analysis</a> can be looked at in terms of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>. There are profound differences in the extent of acceptance of a biological or evolutionary basis for human aggression, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male_Warrior_hypothesis" title="Male Warrior hypothesis">Male Warrior hypothesis</a>,intergroup aggression represents an opportunity for men to gain access to mates, territory, resources and increased status. As such, conflicts may have created selection evolutionary pressures for psychological mechanisms in men to initiate intergroup aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_and_conflict">Violence and conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Violence and conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aggression can involve <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a> that may be <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptive</a> under certain circumstances in terms of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>. This is most obviously the case in terms of attacking prey to obtain food, or in anti-predatory defense. It may also be the case in competition between members of the same species or subgroup, if the average reward (e.g. status, access to resources, protection of self or kin) outweighs average costs (e.g. injury, exclusion from the group, death). There are some hypotheses of specific adaptions for violence in humans under certain circumstances, including for <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a>, but it is often unclear what behaviors may have been selected for and what may have been a byproduct, as in the case of collective violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although aggressive encounters are ubiquitous in the animal kingdom, with often high stakes, most are resolved through posturing, displays and trials of strength. <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">Game theory</a> is used to understand how such behaviors might spread by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> within a population, and potentially become 'Evolutionary Stable Strategies'. An initial model of resolution of conflicts is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawk-dove_game" title="Hawk-dove game">hawk-dove game</a>; others include the <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">Sequential assessment model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">Energetic war of attrition</a>. These try to understand not just one-off encounters but protracted stand-offs, and mainly differ in the criteria by which an individual decides to give up rather than risk loss and harm in physical conflict (such as through estimates of <a href="/wiki/Resource_holding_potential" title="Resource holding potential">resource holding potential</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gender">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gender plays an important role in human aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup> There are multiple theories that seek to explain findings that males and females of the same species can have differing aggressive behaviors. However the conditions under which women and men differ in aggressiveness are not well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In general, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> can be attributed to greater <a href="/wiki/Intraspecific_competition" title="Intraspecific competition">intraspecific competition</a> in one sex, either between rivals for access to mates and/or to be <a href="/wiki/Mate_choice" title="Mate choice">chosen by mates</a>. This may stem from the other gender being constrained by providing greater <a href="/wiki/Parental_investment" title="Parental investment">parental investment</a>, in terms of factors such as <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gamete</a> production, <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">lactation</a>, or upbringing of young. Although there is much variation in species, generally the more physically aggressive sex is the male, particularly in mammals. In species where parental care by both sexes is required, there tends to be less of a difference. When the female can leave the male to care for the offspring, then females may be the larger and more physically aggressive. Competitiveness despite parental investment has also been observed in some species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A related factor is the rate at which males and females are able to mate again after producing offspring, and the basic principles of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> are also influenced by ecological factors affecting the ways or extent to which one sex can compete for the other. The role of such factors in human evolution is controversial. The pattern of male and female aggression is argued to be consistent with evolved sexually-selected behavioral differences, while alternative or complimentary views emphasize conventional <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">social roles</a> stemming from physical evolved differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Aggression in women may have evolved to be, on average, less physically dangerous and more covert or indirect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However, there are critiques for using animal behavior to explain human behavior. Especially in the application of evolutionary explanations to contemporary human behavior, including differences between the genders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the 2015 <i><a href="/wiki/International_Encyclopedia_of_the_Social_%26_Behavioral_Sciences" title="International Encyclopedia of the Social &amp; Behavioral Sciences">International Encyclopedia of the Social &amp; Behavioral Sciences</a></i>, sex differences in aggression is one of the most robust and oldest findings in psychology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274956064_Gender_diff_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-274956064_Gender_diff-49">[49]</a></sup> Past meta-analyses in the encyclopedia found males regardless of age engaged in more physical and verbal aggression while small effect for females engaging in more indirect aggression such as rumor spreading or gossiping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274956064_Gender_diff_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-274956064_Gender_diff-49">[49]</a></sup> It also found males tend to engage in more unprovoked aggression at higher frequency than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274956064_Gender_diff_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-274956064_Gender_diff-49">[49]</a></sup> This analysis also conforms with the <i>Oxford Handbook of Evolutionary Psychology</i> which reviewed past analysis which found men to use more verbal and physical aggression with the difference being greater in the physical type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> There are more recent findings that show that differences in male and female aggression appear at about two years of age, though the differences in aggression are more consistent in middle-aged children and adolescence. Tremblay, Japel and Pérusse (1999) asserted that physically aggressive behaviors such as kicking, biting and hitting are age-typical expressions of innate and spontaneous reactions to biological drives such as anger, hunger, and affiliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Girls' relational aggression, meaning non-physical or indirect, tends to increase after age two while physical aggression decreases. There was no significant difference in aggression between males and females before two years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308-52">[52]</a></sup> A possible explanation for this could be that girls develop language skills more quickly than boys, therefore they have better ways of verbalizing their wants and needs. They are more likely to use communication when trying to retrieve a toy with the words "Ask nicely" or "Say please."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hay_2011_158.E2.80.9375_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_2011_158.E2.80.9375-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the journal of <i>Aggressive Behaviour,</i> analysis across 9 countries found boy reported more in the use of physical aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308-52">[52]</a></sup> At the same time no consistent sex differences emerged within <a href="/wiki/Relational_aggression" title="Relational aggression">relational aggression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Landsford_2012_298.E2.80.93308-52">[52]</a></sup> It has been found that girls are more likely than boys to use reactive aggression and then retract, but boys are more likely to increase rather than to retract their aggression after their first reaction. Studies show girls' aggressive tactics included gossip, ostracism, breaking confidences, and criticism of a victim's clothing, appearance, or personality, whereas boys engage in aggression that involves a direct physical and/or verbal assault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This could be due to the fact that girls' frontal lobes develop earlier than boys, allowing them to self-restrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hay_2011_158.E2.80.9375_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_2011_158.E2.80.9375-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>One factor that shows insignificant differences between male and female aggression is in sports. In sports, the rate of aggression in both contact and non-contact sports is relatively equal. Since the establishment of Title IX, female sports have increased in competitiveness and importance, which could contribute to the evening of aggression and the "need to win" attitude between both sexes. Among sex differences found in adult sports were that females have a higher scale of indirect hostility while men have a higher scale of assault.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Another difference found is that men have up to 20 times higher levels of <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> than women.</p>
<p>Some studies suggest that romantic involvement in adolescence decreases aggression in males and females, but decreases at a higher rate in females. Females will seem more desirable to their mate if they fit in with society and females that are aggressive do not usually fit well in society, they can often be viewed as antisocial. Female aggression is not considered the norm in society and going against the norm can sometimes prevent one from getting a mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, studies have shown that an increasing number of women are getting arrested for domestic violence charges. In many states, women now account for a quarter to a third of all domestic violence arrests, up from less than 10 percent a decade ago. The new statistics reflect a reality documented in research: women are perpetrators as well as victims of family violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> However, another equally possible explanation is a case of improved diagnostics: it has become more acceptable for men to report female domestic violence to the authorities while at the same time actual female domestic violence has not increased at all. This can be the case when men have become less ashamed of reporting female violence against them, therefore an increasing number of women are arrested, although the actual number of violent women remains the same.</p>
<p>Also, males in competitive sports are often advised by their coaches not to be in intimate relationships based on the premises that they become more docile and less aggressive during an athletic event. The circumstances in which males and females experience aggression are also different. A study showed that social anxiety and stress was positively correlated with aggression in males, meaning as stress and social anxiety increases so does aggression. Furthermore, a male with higher social skills has a lower rate of aggressive behavior than a male with lower social skills. In females, higher rates of aggression were only correlated with higher rates of stress. Other than biological factors that contribute to aggression there are physical factors are well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding sexual dimorphism, humans fall into an intermediate group with moderate sex differences in body size but relatively large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testes" title="Testes">testes</a>. This is a typical pattern of primates where several males and females live together in a group and the male faces an intermediate amount of challenges from other males compared to exclusive <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a> but frequent <a href="/wiki/Sperm_competition" title="Sperm competition">sperm competition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2007-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">Evolutionary psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sociobiology" title="Sociobiology">sociobiology</a> have also discussed and produced theories for some specific forms of male aggression such as <a href="/wiki/Sociobiological_theories_of_rape" title="Sociobiological theories of rape">sociobiological theories of rape</a> and theories regarding the <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_effect" title="Cinderella effect">Cinderella effect</a>. Another evolutionary theory explaining gender differences in aggression is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male_Warrior_hypothesis" title="Male Warrior hypothesis">Male Warrior hypothesis</a>, which explains that males have psychologically evolved for intergroup aggression in order to gain access to mates, resources, territory and status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_pathways">Brain pathways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Brain pathways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many researchers focus on the brain to explain aggression. Numerous circuits within both neocortical and subcortical structures play a central role in controlling aggressive behavior, depending on the species, and the exact role of pathways may vary depending on the type of trigger or intention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mammals, the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Periaqueductal_gray" title="Periaqueductal gray">periaqueductal gray</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Midbrain" title="Midbrain">midbrain</a> are critical areas, as shown in studies on cats, rats, and monkeys. These brain areas control the expression of both behavioral and <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic</a> components of aggression in these species, including vocalization. Electrical stimulation of the hypothalamus causes aggressive behavior<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and the hypothalamus has receptors that help determine aggression levels based on their interactions with serotonin and vasopressin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> These midbrain areas have direct connections with both the <a href="/wiki/Brainstem" title="Brainstem">brainstem</a> nuclei controlling these functions, and with structures such as the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a>.</p>
<p>Stimulation of the amygdala results in augmented aggressive behavior in hamsters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesions" title="Lesions">lesions</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">evolutionarily homologous</a> area in the lizard greatly reduce competitive drive and aggression (Bauman et al. 2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhesus_monkeys" title="Rhesus monkeys">rhesus monkeys</a>, neonatal lesions in the amygdala or hippocampus results in reduced expression of social dominance, related to the regulation of aggression and fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Several experiments in attack-primed Syrian golden hamsters, for example, support the claim of circuity within the amygdala being involved in control of aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Potegal.2C_M_1996._pp._869-880_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Potegal.2C_M_1996._pp._869-880-66">[66]</a></sup> The role of the amygdala is less clear in primates and appears to depend more on situational context, with lesions leading to increases in either social affiliatory or aggressive responses.</p>
<p>The broad area of the cortex known as the <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a> (PFC) has been implicated in aggression, along with many other functions. such as including inhibition of emotions. Reduced activity of the prefrontal cortex, in particular its medial and <a href="/wiki/Orbitofrontal_cortex" title="Orbitofrontal cortex">orbitofrontal</a> portions, has been associated with violent/antisocial aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The role of the chemicals in the brain, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurotransmitters" title="Neurotransmitters">neurotransmitters</a>, in aggression has also been examined. This varies depending on the pathway, the context and other factors such as gender. A deficit in <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> has been theorized to have a primary role in causing impulsivity and aggression. At least one epigenetic study supports this supposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Nevertheless, low levels of serotonin transmission may explain a vulnerability to impulsiveness, potential aggression, and may have an effect through interactions with other neurochemical systems. These include <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> systems which are generally associated with attention and motivation toward rewards, and operate at various levels. <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">Norepinephrine</a>, also known as noradrenaline, may influence aggression responses both directly and indirectly through the hormonal system, the <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">sympathetic nervous system</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (including the brain). It appears to have different effects depending on the type of triggering stimulus, for example social isolation/rank versus shock/chemical agitation which appears not to have a linear relationship with aggression. Similarly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a>, although associated with inhibitory functions at many CNS synapses, sometimes shows a positive correlation with aggression, including when potentiated by alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hormonal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropeptides" title="Neuropeptides">neuropeptides</a> <a href="/wiki/Vasopressin" title="Vasopressin">vasopressin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxytocin" title="Oxytocin">oxytocin</a> play a key role in complex social behaviours in many mammals such as regulating attachment, social recognition, and aggression. Vasopressin has been implicated in male-typical social behaviors which includes aggression. Oxytocin may have a particular role in regulating female bonds with offspring and mates, including the use of protective aggression. Initial studies in humans suggest some similar effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>In human, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aggressive_behavior" title="Aggressive behavior">aggressive behavior</a> has been associated with abnormalities in three principal regulatory systems in the body <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">catecholamine systems</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical_axis" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical axis">hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical axis</a>. Abnormalities in these systems also are known to be induced by <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a>, either severe, acute stress or chronic low-grade stress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testosterone">Testosterone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Testosterone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="60" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out into another article titled <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Testosterone_and_aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Testosterone and aggression (page does not exist)">Testosterone and aggression</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Aggression" title="Talk:Aggression">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(May 2015)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Testosterone#Aggression" title="Testosterone">Testosterone § Aggression</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormones" title="Hormones">Hormones</a> are chemicals that circulate in the body to affect cells and the nervous system, including the brain. <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">Testosterone</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Steroid_hormone" title="Steroid hormone">steroid hormone</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Androgen" title="Androgen">androgen</a> group, which is most linked to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prenatal" title="Prenatal">prenatal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postnatal" title="Postnatal">postnatal</a> development of the male gender and physique, which in turn has been linked on average to more physical aggression in many species. Early androgenization as an organizational effect on the developing brains of both males and females, making more neural circuits that control sexual behavior as well as intermale and interfemale aggression become more sensitive to testosterone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson.2C_N._2013_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson.2C_N._2013-74">[74]</a></sup> Thus, aggressive behavior tends to increase with testosterone. There are noticeable sex differences in aggression. Testosterone is present to a lesser extent in females, who may be more sensitive to its effects. Animal studies have also indicated a link between incidents of aggression and the individual level of circulating testosterone. However, results in relation to primates, particularly humans, are less clear cut and are at best only suggestive of a positive association in some contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_goozen_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_goozen-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In humans, there is a seasonal variation in aggression associated with changes in testosterone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer_319.E2.80.93345_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archer_319.E2.80.93345-76">[76]</a></sup> For example, in some primate species, such as rhesus monkeys and baboons, females are more likely to engage in fights around the time of ovulation as well as right before menstruation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson.2C_N._2013_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson.2C_N._2013-74">[74]</a></sup> If the results were the same in humans as they are in rhesus monkeys and baboons, then the increase in aggressive behaviors during ovulation is explained by the decline in estrogen levels. This makes normal testosterone levels more effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Castrated mice and rats exhibit lower levels of aggression. Males castrated as neonates exhibit low levels of aggression even when given testosterone throughout their development.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenge_hypothesis">Challenge hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Challenge hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="696" data-file-width="464" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG/170px-Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG/255px-Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG/340px-Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melospiza_melodia_01450t.JPG" title="Ampliar"></a></div>
Song sparrow</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Challenge_hypothesis" title="Challenge hypothesis">challenge hypothesis</a> outlines the dynamic relationship between plasma testosterone levels and aggression in mating contexts in many species. It proposes that testosterone is linked to aggression when it is beneficial for reproduction, such as in mate guarding and preventing the encroachment of intrasexual rivals. The challenge hypothesis predicts that seasonal patterns in testosterone levels in a species are a function of mating system (monogamy versus polygyny), paternal care, and male-male aggression in <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_breeder" title="Seasonal breeder">seasonal breeders</a>. This pattern between testosterone and aggression was first observed in seasonally breeding birds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Song_sparrow" title="Song sparrow">song sparrow</a>, where testosterone levels rise modestly with the onset of the breeding season to support basic reproductive functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wingfield.2C_John_C..2C_Ball.2C_Gregory_F..2C_Dufty_Jr.2C_Alfred_M..2C_Hegner.2C_Robert_E..2C_Ramenofsky.2C_Marilyn_1987_602.E2.80.93608_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wingfield.2C_John_C..2C_Ball.2C_Gregory_F..2C_Dufty_Jr.2C_Alfred_M..2C_Hegner.2C_Robert_E..2C_Ramenofsky.2C_Marilyn_1987_602.E2.80.93608-78">[78]</a></sup> The hypothesis has been subsequently expanded and modified to predict relationships between testosterone and aggression in other species. For example, chimpanzees, which are continuous breeders, show significantly raised testosterone levels and aggressive male-male interactions when receptive and fertile females are present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Currently, no research has specified a relationship between the modified challenge hypothesis and human behavior, or the human nature of <a href="/wiki/Concealed_ovulation" title="Concealed ovulation">concealed ovulation</a>, although some suggest it may apply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archer_319.E2.80.93345_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Archer_319.E2.80.93345-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_the_nervous_system">Effects on the nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects on the nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1531" data-file-width="4539" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png/220px-Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png/330px-Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png/440px-Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Testosterone_estradiol_conversion.png" title="Ampliar"></a></div>
Testosterone to Estradiol conversion</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another line of research has focused on the proximate effects of circulating testosterone on the nervous system, as mediated by local metabolism within the brain. Testosterone can be metabolized to 17b-<a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">estradiol</a> by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Aromatase" title="Aromatase">aromatase</a>, or to 5-alpha-<a href="/wiki/Dihydrotestosterone" title="Dihydrotestosterone">dihydrotestosterone</a> (DHT) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5a-reductase" title="5a-reductase">5a-reductase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aromatase is highly expressed in regions involved in the regulation of aggressive behavior, such as the amygdala and hypothalamus. In studies using genetic knock-out techniques in inbred mice, male mice that lacked a functional aromatase enzyme displayed a marked reduction in aggression. Long-term treatment with estradiol partially restored aggressive behavior, suggesting that the neural conversion of circulating testosterone to estradiol and its effect on estrogen receptors influences inter-male aggression. In addition, two different estrogen receptors, ERa and ERb, have been identified as having the ability to exert different effects on aggression in mice. However, the effect of estradiol appears to vary depending on the strain of mouse, and in some strains it reduces aggression during long days (16 h of light), while during short days (8 h of light) estradiol rapidly increases aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soma2008_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soma2008-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another hypothesis is that testosterone influences brain areas that control behavioral reactions. Studies in animal models indicate that aggression is affected by several interconnected cortical and subcortical structures within the so-called <a href="/wiki/Social_behavior" title="Social behavior">social behavior</a> network. A study involving lesions and electrical-chemical stimulation in rodents and cats revealed that such a neural network consists of the medial <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, medial <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periaqueductal_grey" title="Periaqueductal grey">periaqueductal grey</a> (PAG), and it positively modulates reactive aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Moreover, a study done in human subjects showed that prefrontal-amygdala connectivity is modulated by endogenous testosterone during social emotional behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In human studies, testosterone-aggression research has also focused on the role of the <a href="/wiki/Orbitofrontal_cortex" title="Orbitofrontal cortex">orbitofrontal cortex</a> (OFC). This brain area is strongly associated with impulse control and self-regulation systems that integrate emotion, motivation, and cognition to guide context-appropriate behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta.2C_P._H..2C_Beer.2C_J._2009_2357.E2.80.932368_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta.2C_P._H..2C_Beer.2C_J._2009_2357.E2.80.932368-83">[83]</a></sup> Patients with localized lesions to the OFC engage in heightened reactive aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Aggressive behavior may be regulated by testosterone via reduced medial OFC engagement following social provocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehta.2C_P._H..2C_Beer.2C_J._2009_2357.E2.80.932368_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mehta.2C_P._H..2C_Beer.2C_J._2009_2357.E2.80.932368-83">[83]</a></sup> When measuring participants' salivary testosterone, higher levels can predict subsequent aggressive behavioral reactions to unfairness faced during a task. Moreover, brain scanning with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FMRI" title="FMRI">fMRI</a> shows reduced activity in the medial OFC during such reactions. Such findings may suggest that a specific brain region, the OFC, is a key factor in understanding reactive aggression.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_associations_with_behavior">General associations with behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: General associations with behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Scientists have for a long time been interested in the relationship between testosterone and aggressive behavior. In most species, males are more aggressive than females. Castration of males usually has a pacifying effect on aggressive behavior in males. In humans, males engage in crime and especially violent crime more than females. The involvement in crime usually rises in the early teens to mid teens which happen at the same time as testosterone levels rise. Research on the relationship between testosterone and aggression is difficult since the only reliable measurement of brain testosterone is by a <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_puncture" title="Lumbar puncture">lumbar puncture</a> which is not done for research purposes. Studies therefore have often instead used more unreliable measurements from blood or saliva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis2009_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2009-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Handbook of Crime Correlates</i>, a review of crime studies, states most studies support a link between adult criminality and testosterone although the relationship is modest if examined separately for each sex. However, nearly all studies of juvenile delinquency and testosterone are not significant. Most studies have also found testosterone to be associated with behaviors or personality traits linked with criminality such as <a href="/wiki/Antisocial_personality_disorder" title="Antisocial personality disorder">antisocial behavior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>. Many studies have also been done on the relationship between more general aggressive behavior/feelings and testosterone. About half the studies have found a relationship and about half no relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis2009_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2009-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies of testosterone levels of male athletes before and after a competition revealed that testosterone levels rise shortly before their matches, as if in anticipation of the competition, and are dependent on the outcome of the event: testosterone levels of winners are high relative to those of losers. No specific response of testosterone levels to competition was observed in female athletes, although a mood difference was noted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID_10097017_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID_10097017-86">[86]</a></sup> In addition, some experiments have failed to find a relationship between testosterone levels and aggression in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The possible correlation between testosterone and aggression could explain the "roid rage" that can result from <a href="/wiki/Anabolic_steroid" title="Anabolic steroid">anabolic steroid</a> use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> although an effect of abnormally high levels of steroids does not prove an effect at physiological levels.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dehydroepiandrosterone">Dehydroepiandrosterone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dehydroepiandrosterone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dehydroepiandrosterone" title="Dehydroepiandrosterone">Dehydroepiandrosterone</a> (DHEA) is the most abundant circulating androgen hormone and can be rapidly metabolized within target tissues into potent androgens and estrogens. Gonadal steroids generally regulate aggression during the breeding season, but non-gonadal steroids may regulate aggression during the non-breeding season. Castration of various species in the non-breeding season has no effect on territorial aggression. In several avian studies, circulating DHEA has been found to be elevated in birds during the non-breeding season. These data support the idea that non-breeding birds combine adrenal and/or gonadal DHEA synthesis with neural DHEA metabolism to maintain territorial behavior when gonadal testosterone secretion is low. Similar results have been found in studies involving different strains of rats, mice, and hamsters. DHEA levels also have been studied in humans and may play a role in human aggression. Circulating DHEAS (its sulfated ester) levels rise during adrenarche (~7 years of age) while plasma testosterone levels are relatively low. This implies that aggression in pre-pubertal children with aggressive conduct disorder might be correlated with plasma DHEAS rather than plasma testosterone, suggesting an important link between DHEAS and human aggressive behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soma2008_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soma2008-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glucocorticoids">Glucocorticoids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Glucocorticoids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">Glucocorticoid</a> hormones have an important role in regulating aggressive behavior. In adult rats, acute injections of <a href="/wiki/Corticosterone" title="Corticosterone">corticosterone</a> promote aggressive behavior and acute reduction of corticosterone decreases aggression; however, a chronic reduction of corticosterone levels can produce abnormally aggressive behavior. In addition, glucocorticoids affect development of aggression and establishment of social hierarchies. Adult mice with low baseline levels of corticosterone are more likely to become dominant than are mice with high baseline corticosterone levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soma2008_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Soma2008-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Glucocorticoids are released by the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">hypothalamic pituitary adrenal</a> (HPA) axis in response to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(biological)" title="Stress (biological)">stress</a>, of which <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> is the most prominent in humans. Results in adults suggest that reduced levels of cortisol, linked to lower fear or a reduced stress response, can be associated with more aggression. However, it may be that proactive aggression is associated with low cortisol levels while reactive aggression may be accompanied by elevated levels. Differences in assessments of cortisol may also explain a diversity of results, particularly in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_goozen_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-van_goozen-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The HPA axis is related to the general <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight-or-flight response</a> or <a href="/wiki/Acute_stress_reaction" title="Acute stress reaction">acute stress reaction</a>, and the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catecholamines" title="Catecholamines">catecholamines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a>, popularly known as adrenaline.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pheromones">Pheromones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pheromones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In many animals, aggression can be linked to <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> released between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecifics" title="Conspecifics">conspecifics</a>. In mice, <a href="/wiki/Major_urinary_proteins" title="Major urinary proteins">major urinary proteins</a> (Mups) have been demonstrated to promote innate aggressive behavior in males,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCreport_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCreport-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamero1_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamero1-93">[93]</a></sup> and can be mediated by neuromodulatory systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Mups activate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfactory_sensory_neuron" title="Olfactory sensory neuron">olfactory sensory neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a> (VNO), a subsystem of the nose known to detect pheromones via specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_receptor" title="Sensory receptor">sensory receptors</a>, of mice<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamero1_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chamero1-93">[93]</a></sup> and rats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krieger1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krieger1-95">[95]</a></sup> Pheremones have also been identified in <a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">fruit flies</a>, detected by neurons in the antenna, that send a message to the brain eliciting aggression; it has been noted that aggression pheremones have not been identified in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetics_of_aggression" title="Genetics of aggression">Genetics of aggression</a></div>
<p>In general, differences in a continuous phenotype such as aggression are likely to result from the action of a large number of genes each of small effect, which interact with each other and the environment through development and life.</p>
<p>In a non-mammalian example of genes related to aggression, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruitless" title="Fruitless">fruitless</a> gene in <a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">fruit flies</a> is a critical determinant of certain sexually dimorphic behaviors, and its artificial alteration can result in a reversal of stereotypically male and female patterns of aggression in fighting. However, in what was thought to be a relatively clear case, inherent complexities have been reported in deciphering the connections between interacting genes in an environmental context and a social <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> involving multiple behavioral and sensory interactions with another organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mice, candidate genes for differentiating aggression between the sexes are the Sry (sex determining region Y) gene, located on the Y chromosome and the Sts (steroid sulfatase) gene. The Sts gene encodes the steroid sulfatase enzyme, which is pivotal in the regulation of neurosteroid biosynthesis. It is expressed in both sexes, is correlated with levels of aggression among male mice, and increases dramatically in females after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parturition" title="Parturition">parturition</a> and during <a href="/wiki/Lactation" title="Lactation">lactation</a>, corresponding to the onset of maternal aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Potegal.2C_M_1996._pp._869-880_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Potegal.2C_M_1996._pp._869-880-66">[66]</a></sup> At least one study has found a possible epigenetic signature (i.e. decreased methylation at a specific CpG site on the promoter region) of the serotonin receptor 5-HT3a that is associated with maternal aggression among human subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In humans, there is good evidence that the basic human neural architecture underpinning the potential for flexible aggressive responses is influenced by genes as well as environment. In terms of variation between individual people, more than 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twin_studies" title="Twin studies">twin and adoption studies</a> have been conducted in recent decades examining the genetic basis of aggressive behavior and related constructs such as conduct disorders. According to a <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> published in 2002, approximately 40% of variation between individuals is explained by differences in genes, and 60% by differences in environment (mainly non-shared environmental influences rather than those that would be shared by being raised together). However, such studies have depended on self-report or observation by others including parents, which complicates interpretation of the results. The few laboratory-based analyses have not found significant amounts of individual variation in aggression explicable by genetic variation in the human population. Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_linkage" title="Genetic linkage">linkage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_association" title="Genetic association">association</a> studies that seek to identify specific genes, for example that influence neurotransmitter or hormone levels, have generally resulted in contradictory findings characterized by failed attempts at replication. One possible factor is an allele (variant) of the <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase_A" title="Monoamine oxidase A">MAO-A gene</a> which, in interaction with certain life events such as childhood maltreatment (which may show a <a href="/wiki/Main_effect" title="Main effect">main effect</a> on its own), can influence development of brain regions such as the amygdala and as a result some types of behavioral response may be more likely. The generally unclear picture has been compared to equally difficult findings obtained in regard to other complex behavioral phenotypes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">Humans</a> share aspects of aggression with non-human animals, and have specific aspects and complexity related to factors such as genetics, early development, social learning and flexibility, culture and morals. Konrad Lorenz stated in his 1963 classic, <i>On Aggression,</i> that human behavior is shaped by four main, survival-seeking animal drives. Taken together, these drives—hunger, fear, reproduction, and aggression—achieve natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> elaborated in <i>On Human Nature</i> that aggression is, typically, a means of gaining control over resources. Aggression is, thus, aggravated during times when high population densities generate resource shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> According to Richard Leakey and his colleagues, aggression in humans has also increased by becoming more interested in ownership and by defending his or her property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> adopted the Seville <a href="/wiki/Seville_Statement_on_Violence" title="Seville Statement on Violence">Statement of Violence</a> in 1989 that refuted claims, by evolutionary scientists, that genetics by itself was the sole cause of aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Social and cultural aspects may significantly interfere with the distinct expression of aggressiveness. For example, a high population density, when associated with a decrease of available resources, might be a significant intervening variable for the occurrence of violent acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-:3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many scholars assert that culture is one factor that plays a role in aggression. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal" title="Tribal">Tribal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Band_society" title="Band society">band</a> societies existing before or outside of modern <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a> have sometimes been depicted as peaceful '<a href="/wiki/Noble_savage" title="Noble savage">noble savages</a>'. The <a href="/wiki/%C7%83Kung_people" title="ǃKung people">ǃKung people</a> were described as 'The Harmless People' in a popular work by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Marshall_Thomas" title="Elizabeth Marshall Thomas">Elizabeth Marshall Thomas</a> in 1958,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> while Lawrence Keeley's 1996 <a href="/wiki/War_Before_Civilization" title="War Before Civilization">War Before Civilization</a> suggested that regular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> without modern technology was conducted by most groups throughout human history, including most <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a> tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Studies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a> show a range of different societies. In general, aggression, conflict and violence sometimes occur, but direct confrontation is generally avoided and conflict is socially managed by a variety of verbal and non-verbal methods. Different rates of aggression or violence, currently or in the past, within or between groups, have been linked to the structuring of societies and environmental conditions influencing factors such as <a href="/wiki/Resource" title="Resource">resource</a> or <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a> acquisition, <a href="/wiki/Land_(economics)" title="Land (economics)">land</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_subsistence_techniques" title="List of subsistence techniques">subsistence</a> techniques, and <a href="/wiki/Population_change" title="Population change">population change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>American psychologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gray_(psychologist)" title="Peter Gray (psychologist)">Peter Gray</a> hypothesizes that band hunter-gatherer societies are able to reduce aggression while maintaining relatively peaceful, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egalitarian" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a> relations between members through various methods, such as fostering a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playful" title="Playful">playful</a> spirit in all areas of life, the use of humor to counter the tendency of any one person to dominate the group, and non-coercive or "indulgent" child-rearing practices. Gray likens hunter-gatherer bands to social play groups, while stressing that such play is not frivolous or even easy at all times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlayFoundation_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlayFoundation-109">[109]</a></sup> According to Gray, "Social play—that is, play involving more than one player—is necessarily egalitarian. It always requires a suspension of aggression and dominance along with a heightened sensitivity to the needs and desires of the other players".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
