<p>Joan Durrant at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manitoba" title="University of Manitoba">University of Manitoba</a> writes that a number of studies have found <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_punishment" title="Physical punishment">physical punishment</a> to be associated with "higher levels of aggression against parents, siblings, peers and spouses", even when controlling for other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessons_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lessons-111">[111]</a></sup> According to Elizabeth Gershoff at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>, the more that children are physically punished, the more likely they are as adults to act violently towards family members, including intimate partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In countries where physical punishment of children is perceived as being more culturally accepted, it is less strongly associated with increased aggression; however, physical punishment has been found to predict some increase in child aggression regardless of culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SocialSciences_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-SocialSciences-113">[113]</a></sup> While these associations do not prove <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a>, a number of <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_study" title="Longitudinal study">longitudinal studies</a> suggest that the experience of physical punishment has a direct causal effect on later aggressive behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessons_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lessons-111">[111]</a></sup> In examining several longitudinal studies that investigated the path from disciplinary <a href="/wiki/Spanking" title="Spanking">spanking</a> to aggression in children from preschool age through adolescence, Gershoff concluded: "Spanking consistently predicted increases in children's aggression over time, regardless of how aggressive children were when the spanking occurred".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Development_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Development-114">[114]</a></sup> similar results were found by Catherine Taylor at <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Family violence researcher <a href="/wiki/Murray_A._Straus" title="Murray A. Straus">Murray A. Straus</a> argues, "There are many reasons this evidence has been ignored. One of the most important is the belief that spanking is more effective than nonviolent discipline and is, therefore, sometimes necessary, despite the risk of harmful side effects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Analyzing aggression culturally or politically is complicated by the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Labeling_theory" title="Labeling theory">label</a> 'aggressive' can itself be used as a way of asserting a judgement from a particular point of view.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2015)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> Whether a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coercive" title="Coercive">coercive</a> or violent method of social control is perceived as aggression – or as legitimate versus illegitimate aggression – depends on the position of the relevant parties in relation to the social order of their culture. This in turn can relate to factors such as: norms for coordinating actions and dividing resources; what is considered self-defense or provocation; attitudes towards 'outsiders', attitudes towards specific groups such as women, the disabled or the lower status; the availability of alternative conflict resolution strategies; <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> interdependence and <a href="/wiki/Collective_security" title="Collective security">collective security</a> pacts; fears and impulses; and ultimate goals regarding material and social outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond04_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bond04-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cross-cultural" title="Cross-cultural">Cross-cultural</a> research has found differences in attitudes towards aggression in different cultures. In one questionnaire study of university students, in addition to men overall justifying some types of aggression more than women, United States respondents justified defensive physical aggression more readily than <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> respondents, whereas Japanese students preferred direct verbal aggression (but not indirect) more than their American and Spanish counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Within American culture, <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">southern</a> men were shown in a study on university students to be more affected and to respond more aggressively than northerners when randomly insulted after being bumped into, which was theoretically related to a traditional <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_honor_(Southern_United_States)" title="Culture of honor (Southern United States)">culture of honor in the Southern United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> A similar sociological concept that may be applied in different cultures is <a href="/wiki/Face_(sociological_concept)" title="Face (sociological concept)">'face'</a>. Other cultural themes sometimes applied to the study of aggression include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualistic" title="Individualistic">individualistic</a> versus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivist" title="Collectivist">collectivist</a> styles, which may relate, for example, to whether disputes are responded to with open competition or by accommodating and <a href="/wiki/Conflict_avoidance" title="Conflict avoidance">avoiding conflicts</a>. In a study including 62 countries school principals reported aggressive student behavior more often the more individualist, and hence less collectivist, their country's culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Other comparisons made in relation to aggression or war include <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_systems" title="Political systems">political systems</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egalitarian" title="Egalitarian">egalitarian</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratified</a> societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bond04_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bond04-117">[117]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economic system</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> has been viewed by some as reliant on the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">leveraging</a> of human competitiveness and aggression in pursuit of resources and trade, which has been considered in both positive and negative terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Attitudes about the social acceptability of particular acts or targets of aggression are also important factors. This can be highly controversial, as for example in disputes between religions or nation states, for example in regard to the <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_conflict" title="Arab–Israeli conflict">Arab–Israeli conflict</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amjad_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-amjad-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_violence_research" title="Media violence research">Media violence research</a></div>
<p>Some scholars believe that behaviors like aggression may be partially learned by watching and imitating people's behavior, while other researchers have concluded that the media may have some small effects on aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> There is also research questioning this view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> For instance, a long-term outcome study of youth found no long-term relationship between playing violent video games and youth violence or bullying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> One study suggested there is a smaller effect of violent video games on aggression than has been found with television violence on aggression. This effect is positively associated with type of game violence and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_relationship" title="Inverse relationship">negatively associated</a> to time spent playing the games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The author concluded that insufficient evidence exists to link video game violence with aggression. However, another study suggested links to aggressive behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fear-induced_aggression">Fear-induced aggression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Fear-induced aggression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to philosopher and neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Nayef_Al-Rodhan" title="Nayef Al-Rodhan">Nayef Al-Rodhan</a>, "fear(survival)-induced <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Preemptive_aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Preemptive aggression (page does not exist)">pre-emptive aggression</a>" is a human reaction to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injustices" title="Injustices">injustices</a> that are perceived to threaten <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/survival" title="wikt:survival">survival</a>. It is often the root of the unthinkable <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">brutality</a> and injustice perpetuated by human beings. It may occur at any time, even in situations that appear to be calm and under control. Where there is injustice that is perceived as posing a threat to survival, "fear(survival)-induced pre-emptive aggression" will result in individuals taking whatever action necessary to be free from that threat.</p>
<p>Nayef Al-Rodhan argues that humans' strong tendency towards "fear(survival)-induced pre-emptive aggression" means that situations of <a href="/wiki/Anarchy" title="Anarchy">anarchy</a> or near anarchy should be prevented at all costs. This is because anarchy provokes <a href="/wiki/Fear" title="Fear">fear</a>, which in turn results in aggression, brutality, and <a href="/wiki/Injustice" title="Injustice">injustice</a>. Even in non-anarchic situations, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Survival_Instinct" title="Survival Instinct">survival instincts</a> and fear can be very powerful forces, and they may be incited instantaneously. "Fear(survival)-induced pre-emptive aggression" is one of the key factors that may push naturally amoral humans to behave in immoral ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Knowing this, Al-Rodhan maintains that we must prepare for the circumstances that may arise from humans' aggressive behavior. According to Al-Rodhan, the risk of this aggression and its ensuing brutality should be minimized through <a href="/wiki/Confidence-building_measures" title="Confidence-building measures">confidence-building measures</a> and policies that promote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclusiveness" title="Inclusiveness">inclusiveness</a> and prevent <a href="/wiki/Anarchy" title="Anarchy">anarchy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The frequency of physical aggression in humans peaks at around 2–3 years of age. It then declines gradually on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> These observations suggest that physical aggression is not only a learned behavior but that development provides opportunities for the learning and biological development of self-regulation. However, a small subset of children fail to acquire all the necessary self-regulatory abilities and tend to show atypical levels of physical aggression across development. These may be at risk for later violent behavior or, conversely, lack of aggression that may be considered necessary within society. Some findings suggest that early aggression does not necessarily lead to aggression later on, however, although the course through early childhood is an important predictor of outcomes in middle childhood. In addition, physical aggression that continues is likely occurring in the context of family adversity, including socioeconomic factors. Moreover, 'opposition' and 'status violations' in childhood appear to be more strongly linked to social problems in adulthood than simply aggressive antisocial behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Social learning through interactions in early childhood has been seen as a building block for levels of aggression which play a crucial role in the development of peer relationships in middle childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Overall, an interplay of biological, social and environmental factors can be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Typical expectations</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Young children preparing to enter kindergarten need to develop the socially important skill of being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assertive" title="Assertive">assertive</a>. Examples of assertiveness include asking others for information, initiating conversation, or being able to respond to <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">peer pressure</a>.</li>
<li>In contrast, some young children use aggressive behavior, such as hitting or biting, as a form of communication.</li>
<li>Aggressive behavior can impede learning as a skill deficit, while assertive behavior can facilitate learning. However, with young children, aggressive behavior is developmentally appropriate and can lead to opportunities of building conflict resolution and communication skills.</li>
<li>By school age, children should learn more socially appropriate forms of communicating such as expressing themselves through verbal or written language; if they have not, this behavior may signify a disability or developmental delay.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Aggression triggers</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phobia" title="Phobia">Physical fear of others</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dysfunctional_family" title="Dysfunctional family">Family difficulties</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Learning_disorder" title="Learning disorder">Learning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">neurological</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Conduct_disorder" title="Conduct disorder">conduct/behavior disorders</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">Psychological trauma</a></li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bobo_doll_experiment" title="Bobo doll experiment">Bobo doll experiment</a> was conducted by Albert Bandura in 1961. In this work, Bandura found that children exposed to an aggressive adult model acted more aggressively than those who were exposed to a nonaggressive adult model. This experiment suggests that anyone who comes in contact with and interacts with children can affect the way they react and handle situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Summary points from recommendations by national associations</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Pediatrics" title="American Academy of Pediatrics">American Academy of Pediatrics</a> (2011): "The best way to prevent aggressive behavior is to give your child a stable, secure home life with firm, loving discipline and full-time supervision during the toddler and preschool years. Everyone who cares for your child should be a good role model and agree on the rules he's expected to observe as well as the response to use if he disobeys."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_School_Psychologists" title="National Association of School Psychologists">National Association of School Psychologists</a> (2008): "Proactive aggression is typically reasoned, unemotional, and focused on acquiring some goal. For example, a bully wants peer approval and victim submission, and gang members want status and control. In contrast, reactive aggression is frequently highly emotional and is often the result of biased or deficient cognitive processing on the part of the student."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_2">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Gender">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_and_psychology" title="Sex and psychology">Sex and psychology</a></div>
<p>Gender is a factor that plays a role in both human and animal aggression. Males are historically believed to be generally more physically aggressive than females from an early age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> and men commit the vast majority of murders (Buss 2005). This is one of the most robust and reliable behavioral sex differences, and it has been found across many different age groups and cultures. However, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical_studies" title="Empirical studies">empirical studies</a> have found the discrepancy in male and female aggression to be more pronounced in childhood and the gender difference in adults to be modest when studied in an experimental context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Still, there is evidence that males are quicker to aggression (Frey et al. 2003) and more likely than females to express their aggression physically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> When considering indirect forms of non-violent aggression, such as <a href="/wiki/Relational_aggression" title="Relational aggression">relational aggression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_rejection" title="Social rejection">social rejection</a>, some scientists argue that females can be quite aggressive, although female aggression is rarely expressed physically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> An exception is <a href="/wiki/Intimate_partner_violence" title="Intimate partner violence">intimate partner violence</a> that occurs among couples who are engaged, married, or in some other form of intimate relationship. In such cases, some research suggests that women are more physically aggressive than men, although differences are small and men are less likely to be injured than women are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although females are less likely than males to initiate physical violence, they can express aggression by using a variety of non-physical means. Exactly which method women use to express aggression is something that varies from culture to culture. On <a href="/wiki/Bellona_Island" title="Bellona Island">Bellona Island</a>, a culture based on male dominance and physical <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a>, women tend to get into conflicts with other women more frequently than with men. When in conflict with males, instead of using physical means, they make up songs mocking the man, which spread across the island and humiliate him. If a woman wanted to kill a man, she would either convince her male relatives to kill him or hire an assassin. Although these two methods involve physical violence, both are forms of indirect aggression, since the aggressor herself avoids getting directly involved or putting herself in immediate physical danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bjorkqvist_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-bjorkqvist-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>See also the sections on <a href="/wiki/Aggression#Testosterone" title="Aggression">testosterone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aggression#Evolutionary_explanations" title="Aggression">evolutionary explanations</a> for gender differences above.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Situational_factors">Situational factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Situational factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stereotype_threat" title="Stereotype threat">Stereotype threat</a></div>
<p>There has been some links between those prone to violence and their alcohol use. Those who are prone to violence and use alcohol are more likely to carry out violent acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Alcohol impairs judgment, making people much less cautious than they usually are (MacDonald et al. 1996). It also disrupts the way information is processed (Bushman 1993, 1997; Bushman &amp; Cooper 1990).</p>
<p>Pain and discomfort also increase aggression. Even the simple act of placing one's hands in hot water can cause an aggressive response. Hot temperatures have been implicated as a factor in a number of studies. One study completed in the midst of the civil rights movement found that riots were more likely on hotter days than cooler ones (Carlsmith &amp; Anderson 1979). Students were found to be more aggressive and irritable after taking a test in a hot classroom (Anderson et al. 1996, Rule, et al. 1987). Drivers in cars without air conditioning were also found to be more likely to honk their horns (Kenrick &amp; MacFarlane 1986), which is used as a measure of aggression and has shown links to other factors such as generic symbols of aggression or the visibility of other drivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frustration is another major cause of aggression. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frustration_aggression_theory" title="Frustration aggression theory">Frustration aggression theory</a> states that aggression increases if a person feels that he or she is being blocked from achieving a goal (Aronson et al. 2005). One study found that the closeness to the goal makes a difference. The study examined people waiting in line and concluded that the 2nd person was more aggressive than the 12th one when someone cut in line (Harris 1974). Unexpected frustration may be another factor. In a separate study to demonstrate how unexpected frustration leads to increased aggression, Kulik &amp; Brown (1979) selected a group of students as volunteers to make calls for charity donations. One group was told that the people they would call would be generous and the collection would be very successful. The other group was given no expectations. The group that expected success was more upset when no one was pledging than the group who did not expect success (everyone actually had horrible success). This research suggests that when an expectation does not materialize (successful collections), unexpected frustration arises which increases aggression.</p>
<p>There is some evidence to suggest that the presence of violent objects such as a gun can trigger aggression. In a study done by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Berkowitz" title="Leonard Berkowitz">Leonard Berkowitz</a> and Anthony Le Page (1967), college students were made angry and then left in the presence of a gun or badminton racket. They were then led to believe they were delivering electric shocks to another student, as in the <a href="/wiki/Milgram_experiment" title="Milgram experiment">Milgram experiment</a>. Those who had been in the presence of the gun administered more shocks. It is possible that a violence-related stimulus increases the likelihood of aggressive cognitions by activating the <a href="/wiki/Semantic_network" title="Semantic network">semantic network</a>.</p>
<p>A new proposal links military experience to anger and aggression, developing aggressive reactions and investigating these effects on those possessing the traits of a serial killer. Castle and Hensley state, "The military provides the social context where servicemen learn aggression, violence, and murder."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">Post-traumatic stress disorder</a> (PTSD) is also a serious issue in the military, also believed to sometimes lead to aggression in soldiers who are suffering from what they witnessed in battle. They come back to the civilian world and may still be haunted by flashbacks and nightmares, causing severe stress. In addition, it has been claimed that in the rare minority who are claimed to be inclined toward serial killing, violent impulses may be reinforced and refined in war, possibly creating more effective murderers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_positive_adaptation_theory">As a positive adaptation theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Aggression&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: As a positive adaptation theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some recent scholarship has questioned traditional psychological conceptualizations of aggression as universally negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009-30">[30]</a></sup> Most traditional psychological definitions of aggression focus on the harm to the recipient of the aggression, implying this is the intent of the aggressor; however this may not always be the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith.2C_2007_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith.2C_2007-152">[152]</a></sup> From this alternate view, although the recipient may or may not be harmed, the perceived intent is to increase the status of the aggressor, not necessarily to harm the recipient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawley_.26_Vaughn.2C_2003_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawley_.26_Vaughn.2C_2003-153">[153]</a></sup> Such scholars contend that traditional definitions of aggression have no validity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>From this view, rather than concepts such as assertiveness, aggression, violence and criminal violence existing as distinct constructs, they exist instead along a continuum with moderate levels of aggression being most adaptive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson_.26_Beaver.2C_2009-30">[30]</a></sup> Such scholars do not consider this a trivial difference, noting that many traditional researchers' aggression measurements may measure outcomes lower down in the continuum, at levels which are adaptive, yet they generalize their findings to non-adaptive levels of aggression, thus losing precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson.2C_2010_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson.2C_2010-154">[154]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-Krah.C3.A92013-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Krah.C3.A92013_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Barbara Krahé (11 February 2013). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=vUg5NGU2lEcC&amp;pg=PT16" rel="nofollow"><i>The Social Psychology of Aggression: 2nd Edition</i></a>.
